  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8dff79bd-4a72-40a3-bc54-216494d28a7c'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<span id="freeTextContainer11750071669082621922">On October 12, 1972, a plane carrying a team of young rugby players crashed into the remote, snow-peaked Andes.   <span id="freeText11750071669082621922" style="display:none">On October 12, 1972, a plane carrying a team of young rugby players crashed into the remote, snow-peaked Andes. Out of the forty-five original passengers and crew, only sixteen made it off the mountain alive. For ten excruciating weeks they suffered deprivations beyond imagining, confronting nature head-on at its most furious and inhospitable. And to survive, they were forced to do what would have once been unthinkable...<br /><br />This is their story—one of the most astonishing true adventures of the twentieth century.</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This the original book that announced to the world the tragedy of the Andes survivors in all detail, whereas the Parrado book is just his memoir of th<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This the original book that announced to the world the tragedy of the Andes survivors in all detail, whereas the Parrado book is just his memoir of the event written many years afterwards. For me, is one of the best book I ever read, and I've read it many times over, both in Spanish and English. In fact, I'm thinking of buying the kindle version and read it one more time. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='questionText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This question contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/429806-the-book-that-puts-you-at-the-scene-of"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The book, that puts you at the scene of the crash that left a Uruguayan rugby team stranded in the Andes mountains. This book that is practically about feats of cannibalism, in which people are eating there dead friends is a bit gut wrenching.<br /> <br />The theme of this book is that, all humans have it in them to survive, to see there families again, and if they try hard enough they will. What do you think about this? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>
</span>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/7203272-deerae"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1359931035i/7203272._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/7203272-deerae">DeeRae</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I don't necessarily think that's the theme, "..all humans have it in them to survive...". Many that had resorted to cannibalism died. I think it was s<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I don't necessarily think that's the theme, "..all humans have it in them to survive...". Many that had resorted to cannibalism died. I think it was simply telling their story. It also may have been a way to reintroduce them back into society and their own everyday lives. If an author can put you into the mind of the characters, in this case the team, you can rationalize their choices, their actions. Perhaps it paved the way and provided understanding for society to allow them to resume normal functioning and not be perceived as monsters for resorting to cannibalism. Or maybe it was a money maker? I haven't researched the motives, and it was before my time ;) <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_1098807426" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1098807426" />
    <a title="Idarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1283951-idarah"><img alt="Idarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596863879p2/1283951.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1098807426?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Idarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Idarah" href="/user/show/1283951-idarah">Idarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13633291-alive">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1283951-idarah?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1283951-idarah?shelf=memoir-bio">memoir-bio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1098807426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4509712074276299380"><blockquote>
  <b>"[The survivors] had neither sensationalized nor sentimentalized their own experience and it seemed important for me to tell the reader what they had told me in the same 'matter-of-fact' manner." –Piers Paul Read</b>
</blockquote><br/>I remember watching the film adaptation of this book when I was quite young, and being so impressed with the resilience of the human spirit, and the desire to live. This book surpassed the film, because Read did such a great job of involving the reader in the whole ordeal, including </span>
  <span id="freeText4509712074276299380" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>"[The survivors] had neither sensationalized nor sentimentalized their own experience and it seemed important for me to tell the reader what they had told me in the same 'matter-of-fact' manner." –Piers Paul Read</b>
</blockquote><br />I remember watching the film adaptation of this book when I was quite young, and being so impressed with the resilience of the human spirit, and the desire to live. This book surpassed the film, because Read did such a great job of involving the reader in the whole ordeal, including the plane crash survivors, their families, and the efforts others made to keep searching for the victims even when the odds of survival were dismal. <br /><br />This edition had interviews with the author and two survivors thirty years after the publication of the book. It's really hard for me to believe that Read was only thirty-one years old when he was selected for this great project, even though he'd previously only written fictional novels. I also love that it was extremely fact based. Nowhere in this book is the reader told what they should feel about sensitive subject matter, and yet it was told in such a way that I felt involved–a spectator and visitor to the stranded fuselage that served as home to the survivors. I'm glad I read this before I read Nando Parrado's personal memoir about the ordeal, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/454236.Miracle_in_The_Andes" title="Miracle in The Andes by Nando Parrado" rel="nofollow noopener">Miracle in The Andes</a>, although it will probably be some time before I can recircle this event. It really moved me to the core. Definitely a compelling read. Inspirational and gut wrenching. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>"We all have our own mountains, and it's important to remember that no matter how bad things are, one can always overcome them and more so, one must never forget that they can always be worse. It's important to value the small things in life." –Alvaro Mangino, one of the sixteen survivors</b>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="4509712074276299380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_72919970" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72919970" />
    <a title="Hannah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2411388-hannah"><img alt="Hannah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1272561978p2/2411388.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72919970?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah" href="/user/show/2411388-hannah">Hannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=2011-reads">2011-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72919970" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12937103007913857157">In October of 1972, a chartered plane carrying 45 passengers and crew left Uruguay to travel to Chile. A majority of the passengers were made up of young men who were part of an amateur rugby team going to Chile for a game. Others included family and friends. Over the rugged Andes, the pilot made a fatal error, and the plane crashed into the side of a mountain, flinging parts of the tail section, fuselage, wing, rudder and even some passengers out over the desolate landscape. The survivors were,</span>
  <span id="freeText12937103007913857157" style="display:none">In October of 1972, a chartered plane carrying 45 passengers and crew left Uruguay to travel to Chile. A majority of the passengers were made up of young men who were part of an amateur rugby team going to Chile for a game. Others included family and friends. Over the rugged Andes, the pilot made a fatal error, and the plane crashed into the side of a mountain, flinging parts of the tail section, fuselage, wing, rudder and even some passengers out over the desolate landscape. The survivors were, for the most part, very young men (average age around 23 years old). On average, they came from priviledged families. Most were devout Catholics. They enjoyed their cigarettes. They loved their mothers and girlfriends. They loved the game of rugby and were eager to experience a taste of the world outside their beloved Uruguay.<br /><br />Over the next 70 days, the remaining survivors battled cold, avalanches, injury, fear and hunger. To survive, they prayed - alot. They devised plans for capturing water. They made forays into the vast white bleak landscape to search for supplies and a way out. They became makeshift doctors and surgeons and helped the wounded. They waited for rescue to come from the outside. And to fight off starvation, they ate their dead.<br /><br />The story of the 16 remaining Andes survivors makes for riveting reading. The first time I read this book I was in my early 20s myself, and I remember the cannibalism being the overriding memory I took away from this book. Now I'm older, and it's not the cannibalism that captures my attention, but how these very young men kept their sanity, faith and courage in the face of unimaginable horrors. Of their cannibalism, they are unapologetic (which is as it should be). However, they didn't take what they did to survive lightly, and one of the survivors says it best:<br /><br /><i>"When one awakes in the morning amid the silence of the mountains and sees all around the snow-capped peaks--it is majestic, sensational, something frightening--one feels alone, alone, alone in the world but for the presence of God. For I can assure you that God is there. We all felt it, inside ourselves, and not because we were the kind of pious youths who are always praying all day long, even though we had a religious education. Not at all. But there one feels the presence of God. One feels, above all, what is called the hand of God, and allows oneself to be guided by it...And when the moment came when we did not have any more food, or anything of that kind, we thought to ourselves that if Jesus at His last supper had shared His flesh and blood with His apostles, then it was a sign to us that we should do the same--take the flesh and blood as an intimate communion between us all. It was this that helped us to survive, and now we do not want this--which was something intimate, intimate--to be hackneyed or touched or anything like that...."</i><br /><br /><i>Alive</i> is much much more then a survival story. It is a glimpse of courage and faith in the midst of death, fear, and hopelessness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12937103007913857157" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_181153842" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/181153842" />
    <a title="Patti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3197487-patti"><img alt="Patti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275236342p2/3197487.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/181153842?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patti" href="/user/show/3197487-patti">Patti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181153842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10214908211814828930">Not gonna lie--I read this book because I wanted to read about how they ate the people. That is what hooked everyone to this story, isn't it? I saw the movie to see how they ate the people. It's what everyone remembers and why we remember the Donner party all these years later. Dude, they ATE THE PEOPLE!!!!!!!<br/><br/>In the book, they had already eaten the first people by about page 70; the book is hundreds of pages longer. Huh, I thought. What are they going to talk about for the rest of the book?<br/><br/>What </span>
  <span id="freeText10214908211814828930" style="display:none">Not gonna lie--I read this book because I wanted to read about how they ate the people. That is what hooked everyone to this story, isn't it? I saw the movie to see how they ate the people. It's what everyone remembers and why we remember the Donner party all these years later. Dude, they ATE THE PEOPLE!!!!!!!<br /><br />In the book, they had already eaten the first people by about page 70; the book is hundreds of pages longer. Huh, I thought. What are they going to talk about for the rest of the book?<br /><br />What they talk about are the other aspects of survival and it is a very compelling read. There was an avalanche shortly after the initial crash, there are a couple of treks to find the tail and to see who is hardy enough to attempt a walk for help. There are deaths and fights and camaraderie and heartbreaks and survival and yes, they eat the people. <br /><br />This of course begs the question of how far any of us would go to survive. Would I be able to take a piece of glass and cut the flesh off of a recently dead human being? I don't think there is any way to answer that without actually being in that situation which, God willing, I never will be. And speaking of God, the boys' faith in God is awe inspiring. I sometimes snap at God when I get caught in traffic and these boys were faithful throughout (although they, understandably, questioned why some lived while others died). I will have to remember this story next time I get snappy.<br /><br />The only reason that I didn't give this book 5 stars is that I found the parts describing the parents' efforts to find the boys rather dull. I don't know if I just anxious to get back onto the mountain with the boys, but I found myself skimming those parts. I will say though that the reunions with the families were just amazing...I can't imagine what the families went through and how full of awe they were to see their sons again.<br /><br />One other thing I would have liked is to some sort of follow up to tell me what the rest of their lives turned out like, especially the older man who had the 4 kids and the boy whose sister and mother died in the crash (or shortly thereafter). Nevertheless, this was a compelling read...I would suggest reading it in the summer though because parts of it made me feel kinda chilly!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10214908211814828930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2200174112" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2200174112" />
    <a title="Myrna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8789819-myrna"><img alt="Myrna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500745789p2/8789819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2200174112?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Myrna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Myrna" href="/user/show/8789819-myrna">Myrna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8789819-myrna?shelf=books-to-screen">books-to-screen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2200174112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2689288575029578241">I was a little obsessed with the movie Survive!, the first version of this story when I was a young girl while my younger brother was appalled. I’ve also seen documentaries and the newer version of the movie Alive in the 90s. Now, finally I've read the book! I'm glad I did! What a shocking story of survival, courage, endurance, and spirituality. This book is tragic but uplifting in many ways as ".... the ordeal in the mountain had changed their attitude towards life...left only with what they tr</span>
  <span id="freeText2689288575029578241" style="display:none">I was a little obsessed with the movie Survive!, the first version of this story when I was a young girl while my younger brother was appalled. I’ve also seen documentaries and the newer version of the movie Alive in the 90s. Now, finally I've read the book! I'm glad I did! What a shocking story of survival, courage, endurance, and spirituality. This book is tragic but uplifting in many ways as ".... the ordeal in the mountain had changed their attitude towards life...left only with what they truly cared for, their families, their novias, their faith in God, and their homeland."</span>

  <span id="freeText5985136216770857812" style="display:none">This is not a story. This is an account, a confession, a testimony but this is a not a story.<br /><br />After finishing reading the last page this morning, I've given this book a great deal of thought and, personal feelings aside, when asked to rate someone's REAL struggles in life I can't rate it anything less than the highest mark. These people went though horrors, not all of them survived. This REALLY HAPPENED and it promises you an unadulterated and very personal view of survival with all of its ugly implications in tow. <br /><br />There are lessons here, gems to learn, for those who have the stomach for it.<br /><br />I would recommend this book to be read by everyone, at least once.<br /><br />My only complaint is the sensationalist drivel written by The New York Times splashed over the front page -"Thunderous Entertainment...A Classic Human Adventure..." --- whaaaaat? <br /><br />entertainment!?<br /><br />adventure!? <br /><br />Go f*ck yourself. When is the death and the suffering of others such a thing? <br /><br />I'm sure the dead will disagree with your assessment.</span>

  <span id="freeText16158622553580257006" style="display:none">If you could read just 10 books in rest of your life,this book is worthy of being one of them!<br />This is a book based on reality that shook the conscience of the world in 1970s and even after almost 40 years past the incident,the book makes such a compelling reading!<br />A football team hires a chartered Plane to play a friendly match across the Andes,due to co-piolt's mistake the plane crashes and our story begins,<br />how they managed to survive is one of the all time great stories of them all!<br />They don't have enough food for 72 days obviously,what will they do to survive?<br />Of course our primary instinct as mammals is to survive and the fans and football players alike face this dilemma,<br />before you judge them for what they did to survive,just ask yourself,what would you do if you were facing the same situation?It's such a controversial question and topic but for anyone who loves reality based or mountain hiking adventure type of books,this is a must read..<br />I didn't write much coz in case you haven't read/heard about this real life story,I don't want to give away spoilers,but I guarantee you,you won't be able to keep this book away,you wont be able to put this book down once you start!<br />Once you start you won't stop and that's the bottom line coz Stone Cold said so(Uhh okay,Jenny Jacobs said so!),<br />55555555555555555 out of 5 stars:)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15901630437711667356">I read this when it first came out in PB, so many years ago, mid-70's. I'd give it 5 stars because I still remember it so clearly, but I never wanted to re-read it. It was well done, but pretty gruesome. Stranded for 10 weeks with not much else to eat but dead passengers (some of them team mates) injured, & cold. They tried a number of things, but finally 2 of them managed to walk out & get help.   <span id="freeText15901630437711667356" style="display:none">I read this when it first came out in PB, so many years ago, mid-70's. I'd give it 5 stars because I still remember it so clearly, but I never wanted to re-read it. It was well done, but pretty gruesome. Stranded for 10 weeks with not much else to eat but dead passengers (some of them team mates) injured, &amp; cold. They tried a number of things, but finally 2 of them managed to walk out &amp; get help. It's one of the most incredible stories of survival I've ever read.<br /><br />I wondered what happened to the kids afterward. One of them, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/101310.Nando_Parrado" title="Nando Parrado" rel="nofollow noopener">Nando Parrado</a>, wrote <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/454236.Miracle_in_The_Andes" title="Miracle in The Andes by Nando Parrado" rel="nofollow noopener">Miracle in The Andes</a>. I wonder if it sheds more light on what the rest did. I'm not really sure I want to know. That experience had to scar many of them badly. I hope the press was a bit easier on them in those days. Likely not...<br /><br />There's a pretty good summary on Wikipedia:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1972_Andes_flight_disaster">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1972_And...</a><br /></span>

<div id="review_83510056" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/83510056" />
    <a title="Lobstergirl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl"><img alt="Lobstergirl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380256801p2/1910836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/83510056?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lobstergirl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lobstergirl" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl">Lobstergirl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/9888337-alive">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Jon Bon Jovi</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer83510056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6555602378105657287">The story itself is rather astounding - after a plane crash high in the Andes, which killed most on board (and a subsequent avalanche which killed more), the remaining survivors lived for ten weeks on melted snow, human flesh and organs of the deceased (and bone marrow and even intestinal contents, squeezed out) and almost certainly would have died had not two of them climbed out of the Andes and found a neighboring valley and other humans, a trip which itself took ten days. Read competed with o</span>
  <span id="freeText6555602378105657287" style="display:none">The story itself is rather astounding - after a plane crash high in the Andes, which killed most on board (and a subsequent avalanche which killed more), the remaining survivors lived for ten weeks on melted snow, human flesh and organs of the deceased (and bone marrow and even intestinal contents, squeezed out) and almost certainly would have died had not two of them climbed out of the Andes and found a neighboring valley and other humans, a trip which itself took ten days. Read competed with other, more well known writers, including Gay Talese, for the story; he thinks his youth, his Englishness, and above all his Roman Catholic faith was what got him the job. (Most of the survivors were deeply Catholic and had overcome their resistance to anthropophagy by comparing it to the sacrament of Communion.) There are fascinating details sprinkled throughout, such as what such a diet will do to you (a bad combination of severe constipation and diarrhea), and the survivors wondering whether they ought to hide the partly eaten human remains scattered around the crash site so that their rescuers wouldn't think badly of them. The eventual contact with outside human life and rescuers is quite moving; several of the survivors were so overjoyed at seeing plant life that they began eating flowers and grass. Still, the writing lacks some of the verve of other adventure stories like <i>The Perfect Storm</i> and <i>Into Thin Air</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6555602378105657287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2590937171" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2590937171" />
    <a title="Katherine Addison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36603555-katherine-addison"><img alt="Katherine Addison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415129731p2/36603555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2590937171?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katherine Addison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katherine Addison" href="/user/show/36603555-katherine-addison">Katherine Addison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13633291-alive">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=survival">survival</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2590937171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17511572172206280745">Famous story of the Uruguayan rugby team that survived ten weeks in the Andes, largely because they ate the dead passengers.<br/><br/>This is not a subtle book, nor does it bother with nuance. It's a fast, vivid, and compelling read. It shows its age mostly in its sexism. Women are nurturing and irrational and must be humored and coddled; men are brave and active, and when they're irrational, they know better; probably it's part of this same gender definition that Read always refers to the survivors as "b</span>
  <span id="freeText17511572172206280745" style="display:none">Famous story of the Uruguayan rugby team that survived ten weeks in the Andes, largely because they ate the dead passengers.<br /><br />This is not a subtle book, nor does it bother with nuance. It's a fast, vivid, and compelling read. It shows its age mostly in its sexism. Women are nurturing and irrational and must be humored and coddled; men are brave and active, and when they're irrational, they know better; probably it's part of this same gender definition that Read always refers to the survivors as "boys," even though the youngest of them was 19, this giving them room to be irrational and weak without compromising their manhood. In a book with more nuance, there might have been some discussion about gender performance and the fulcrum between the young men's athleticism ("manly" and active) and their religious beliefs (irrational and emotional and therefore "feminine," and the only locus where it was acceptable in the microculture of the survivors to show weakness), but this is not that book. Since all of the survivors frame their experience as a religious one, and since Read says the thing he had in common with them was their Roman Catholic beliefs, this is really not a book that's going to pick apart the survivors' practice and experience of religion--even if it were a book that had that kind of intellectual apparatus at all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17511572172206280745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer501879192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8441124691494176641">I purchased this book looking for the facts and an account of the Fairchild Andes crash. What I got was an account, religiously biased, lacking certain facts when needed.<br/><br/>Most of the passengers on the plane were related by being part of or supporting the football team of a religious institution. So of course prayer and the talk of miracles would turn up. But when selecting a writing to tell the story they selected a fellow catholic. <br/><br/>I do not believe the author intentionally hid any facts, however</span>
  <span id="freeText8441124691494176641" style="display:none">I purchased this book looking for the facts and an account of the Fairchild Andes crash. What I got was an account, religiously biased, lacking certain facts when needed.<br /><br />Most of the passengers on the plane were related by being part of or supporting the football team of a religious institution. So of course prayer and the talk of miracles would turn up. But when selecting a writing to tell the story they selected a fellow catholic. <br /><br />I do not believe the author intentionally hid any facts, however where there should have been an exploration of the caloric intake of the survivors and a thorough discussion on geographic locations of the wreckage and that of the attempted rescue, there was a bit too much page space given over to discussion on how religion helped the survivors.<br /><br />The facts that I was after I found on wikipedia.<br /><br />One piece that was especially gratiing was that of the end justification of the use of psychics. <br /><br />I would only recommend this book to a reader who was intensely interested in the events and who was christian. Anyone else, look for a more non-biased account of events. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8441124691494176641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer668625061" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10298017494767562826">Update 2/11/2020: Sergio Catalán died today. And the characters and their thoughts and struggles during their 70+ days out there is very intense.   <span id="freeText1201620175282303001" style="display:none">Wow, what an incredible story. I had seen the movie a while back and thought I knew what happened, but the movie doesn't depict half of what went on up there in the Andes. And the characters and their thoughts and struggles during their 70+ days out there is very intense. Along with what really happened from the parent's point of view with trying to get the gov't to send search and rescue people out, because nobody believed there could be anyone still alive, much less 16 of them. And what the parents went through emotionally wondering if any of the 16 were their sons, and the sorrow of finding out theirs did not make it back, and on top of that, could have been one of the ones (the majority of them) who were eaten. Also the social and religious struggle each of them went through when faced with the thought that they would have to eat their dead friends to stay alive for a possible rescue.<br /><br />I read every interview in the back of the book after the story ended, and even looked up some websites as I could not get enough of this unimaginable story.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer477269917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4167357843975911943">This is a brilliant book telling the very human story of a Uruguay rugby team whose plane was wrecked in the freezing desolate high Andes mountains where they were stranded from October to the end of December in 1973. It tells of their survival against all odds. A story of camaraderie, resilience, and expedience. For the survivors, without any prospects of food, their only hope was the horror of eating the frozen remains of their dead teammates or they would all die. The parents never gave up an</span>
  <span id="freeText4167357843975911943" style="display:none">This is a brilliant book telling the very human story of a Uruguay rugby team whose plane was wrecked in the freezing desolate high Andes mountains where they were stranded from October to the end of December in 1973. It tells of their survival against all odds. A story of camaraderie, resilience, and expedience. For the survivors, without any prospects of food, their only hope was the horror of eating the frozen remains of their dead teammates or they would all die. The parents never gave up and mounted searches and consulted clairvoyants. But it was eventually the three fittest young men who bravely set off from the plane which had become their home and with trials and tribulations eventually reached a lone peasant and the 16 surviving boys were rescued. Read this book it is a moving testament to the power of the human spirit </span>
  <a data-text-id="4167357843975911943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_191535323" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/191535323" />
    <a title="Brenda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2337007-brenda"><img alt="Brenda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577750190p2/2337007.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/191535323?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brenda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brenda" href="/user/show/2337007-brenda">Brenda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2337007-brenda?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer191535323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16520092483035450610">On Friday, Oct. 13, 1972 a Fairchild F-227 chartered from the Uruguayan Air Force, carrying a young amateur rugby team and their families and friends from Uruguay slammed into the middle of the Andes Mountains east of Chile. <br/><br/>They had left home with much excitement on Oct 12th, but reports of bad weather in the Andes had put them down for an overnight stay in Mendoza. Most of the young men, with an average age of just 19, had never flown before, never been away from home, so they were extemely ex</span>
  <span id="freeText16520092483035450610" style="display:none">On Friday, Oct. 13, 1972 a Fairchild F-227 chartered from the Uruguayan Air Force, carrying a young amateur rugby team and their families and friends from Uruguay slammed into the middle of the Andes Mountains east of Chile. <br /><br />They had left home with much excitement on Oct 12th, but reports of bad weather in the Andes had put them down for an overnight stay in Mendoza. Most of the young men, with an average age of just 19, had never flown before, never been away from home, so they were extemely excited to be going to Chile. They set off again in the afternoon of the 13th, and contact was lost at approximately 3.30pm that same day.<br /><br />Initially the plane broke up, with the wing hitting the mountain first, and when they hit the ground, the tail separated from the fuselage, with those people at the rear of the plane dying instantly. When the fuselage finally came to rest, there were 32 survivors left from the 45 who had boarded the plane in such high spirits.<br /><br />Some were severely injured, many had broken bones and other injuries. But there were some who were barely injured, and it was these who took the lead to help their friends. With only a little wine, some candy and chocolate, plus many cigarettes to help them survive, they were sure they would be rescued within a matter of a few days, if not sooner. They made their home in the fuselage, erecting shelters around the plane to help them stay warm. It was intensely cold, and they weren't dressed for these temperatures. They had their faith, as they were all Catholics, and felt God was with them up high in the mountains.<br /><br />But as the days moved on to weeks, their numbers went down, first to 27, then after a horrendous avalanche, they lost another 8, taking their number down to 19, and finally 16. The horrors, the human endurance, catastrophes, the shockingly overwhelming nature of it all, will stun you, shock you, and at times revolt you, but the miracle of their survival will stay with you for a long time after you finish this book. <br /><br />This story of the 16 survivors, what they suffered, and how they remained alive is one everyone should read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16520092483035450610" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Catharine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/288401-catharine"><img alt="Catharine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447254694p2/288401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8212373?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catharine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catharine" href="/user/show/288401-catharine">Catharine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/288401-catharine?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8212373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9133659428294186552">One of the survivors in this book is quoted as saying that his experience of surviving following a plane crash in the Andes Mountains was the greatest experience of his life. All of the survivors suffered from severe cold, lack of food, and some had injuries,or infections, The test was severe, and yet, this young man could say that it was his greatest exprience. I think whenever we prove to ourselves how much we can handle, we grow and we can look back and say that the hardship, whatever it was,</span>
  <span id="freeText9133659428294186552" style="display:none">One of the survivors in this book is quoted as saying that his experience of surviving following a plane crash in the Andes Mountains was the greatest experience of his life. All of the survivors suffered from severe cold, lack of food, and some had injuries,or infections, The test was severe, and yet, this young man could say that it was his greatest exprience. I think whenever we prove to ourselves how much we can handle, we grow and we can look back and say that the hardship, whatever it was, has strengthened us and made us better. <br /> The book was well written and had a very interesting subject. The only thing that bothered me is that the author switched back and forth between using a character's first name and last name. As there were initially about 40 passengers on the flight, it was difficult to track exactly who was doing what. A chart at the end of the book of who survived and how each of the others died would have been very helpful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9133659428294186552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer344370716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7963841495075713797">This book has to be one of the best examples of an author able to relate an account containing culturally taboo subject matter without judgment or sensationalism. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/401514.Alive_The_Story_of_the_Andes_Survivors' title='Alive The Story of the Andes Survivors by Piers Paul Read' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alive: The Story of the Andes Survivors</a> recounts the survival story of a rugby team whose plane crashes in the Andes Mountains. Those who live find themselves confronted with awful choices that no human would want to make. Given up for dead by most of the world and even some of their families, they exist despite little shelter from the</span>
  <span id="freeText7963841495075713797" style="display:none">This book has to be one of the best examples of an author able to relate an account containing culturally taboo subject matter without judgment or sensationalism. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/401514.Alive_The_Story_of_the_Andes_Survivors" title="Alive The Story of the Andes Survivors by Piers Paul Read" rel="nofollow noopener">Alive: The Story of the Andes Survivors</a> recounts the survival story of a rugby team whose plane crashes in the Andes Mountains. Those who live find themselves confronted with awful choices that no human would want to make. Given up for dead by most of the world and even some of their families, they exist despite little shelter from the cold, avalanches and with the knowledge that no search party will likely see them from the air or even have an idea where they crashed.<br /><br />This book is worth the read, but warn that some of the content is not recommended for the squeamish.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_119772191" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119772191" />
    <a title="Corinne Edwards" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/875001-corinne-edwards"><img alt="Corinne Edwards" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569257331p2/875001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/119772191?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corinne Edwards" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corinne Edwards" href="/user/show/875001-corinne-edwards">Corinne Edwards</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=the-nook-book-club">the-nook-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=survival">survival</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=aviation">aviation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=tragedy">tragedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=plane-crash">plane-crash</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/875001-corinne-edwards?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119772191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14854086964007114232">When you know from the beginning of a book that a plane full of young Rugby players crash lands in the snowy Andes mountains, and yet somehow some of those boys survive for weeks and weeks - you know it's not going to be a pretty story. And it's not. It's survival at its grittiest core, what do we humans really need to stay alive? Their story is told in an incredibly straightforward, almost newspaper-story type narrative. There's no real emotion. There's no flowery speech. It's just as true of a</span>
  <span id="freeText14854086964007114232" style="display:none">When you know from the beginning of a book that a plane full of young Rugby players crash lands in the snowy Andes mountains, and yet somehow some of those boys survive for weeks and weeks - you know it's not going to be a pretty story. And it's not. It's survival at its grittiest core, what do we humans really need to stay alive? Their story is told in an incredibly straightforward, almost newspaper-story type narrative. There's no real emotion. There's no flowery speech. It's just as true of a retelling as you can get under the circumstances and if I'm going to read non-fiction, that's exactly how I like it.<br /><br />What they go through on that mountain is so grueling: the terror, the fear, the frustration, the pain and hunger and the bone biting cold. And yet what's interesting is their faith throughout, their actual, real belief that they will be saved despite the deck being stacked against them. They get creative and turn the carcass of the plane into a shelter, they go on expeditions to find out where they are and look for help, they make their own blankets and sleeping bags and perform emergency medical procedures on each other. They eat everything that could possibly be eaten until there is nothing left but the bodies of their comrades that are frozen out in the snow. In order to stay alive, those are eaten too. It does make one squeamish, to read the grisly details of their meals and I keep asking myself if I could do it. Would I do whatever it took to be with my children again?<br /><br />Regardless of what I would do, this story did make for some very interesting reading. I could NOT for the life of me keep all the foreign names of those boys and their parents straight, though. It drove me loco. I finally gave up and by the end I knew who maybe five of them really were, but honestly, it didn't matter that much. The author did his best.<br /><br />No, this story is not for the faint of heart and sometimes, of course, people let their weaknesses get the better of them. But I found this incredible sense of triumph. Human beings are absolutely astonishing creatures and it's amazing how deep that survival instinct can burn. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14854086964007114232" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_129833856" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129833856" />
    <a title="Austin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4259066-austin"><img alt="Austin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289246685p2/4259066.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129833856?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Austin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Austin" href="/user/show/4259066-austin">Austin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129833856" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15491676919211920040">This book is one of those novels that you will never forget. It is a phenomenal writing piece based on a rugby team whose plane crashed when flying over the Andes Mountains. They were stuck in these mountains for over ten weeks. Forty five passengers and the crew were on the plane before it crashed, and only sixteen of the passengers left the mountain alive. This book will go down as one of my all-time favorite novels in the entire world. Since it is a survival story, at any point in time someth</span>
  <span id="freeText15491676919211920040" style="display:none">This book is one of those novels that you will never forget. It is a phenomenal writing piece based on a rugby team whose plane crashed when flying over the Andes Mountains. They were stuck in these mountains for over ten weeks. Forty five passengers and the crew were on the plane before it crashed, and only sixteen of the passengers left the mountain alive. This book will go down as one of my all-time favorite novels in the entire world. Since it is a survival story, at any point in time something dramatic could change. I definitely recommend this book to any reader out there that likes a good thriller novel. But if you are one of those people that get really sad and caught up in the story when someone dies, then this may not be the story for you. But if you can make it through the book until it ends I think everyone out there would be shocked at how perfect this piece is. The author combines the quality imagery with the dramatic intensity of the situation to make the absolute perfect novel. He really brings the emotions out when describing the events that occur in this novel. And after reading this I will always know that at any time I could lose my life so i really need to take advantage of the time I have been given. This book really makes you think about how lucky we are. And that is why I will never forget this story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15491676919211920040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58126379" />
    <a title="Andy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2375228-andy"><img alt="Andy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280847177p2/2375228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58126379?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy" href="/user/show/2375228-andy">Andy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2375228-andy?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2375228-andy?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58126379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5715188090075986064">One of my two favorite books. (The other is To Kill a Mockingbird.) I read this three summers in a row as a teenager, and probably another three times as an adult. And if I live long enough I will read it as many times again. It's simply the greatest survival story ever. I would read this book on camping trips in 90+ degree temperatures, and it would never fail to whisk me away to the Andes.<br/><br/>Here are my only two complaints.... First, I always skip the first chapter, which concerns the history of </span>
  <span id="freeText5715188090075986064" style="display:none">One of my two favorite books. (The other is To Kill a Mockingbird.) I read this three summers in a row as a teenager, and probably another three times as an adult. And if I live long enough I will read it as many times again. It's simply the greatest survival story ever. I would read this book on camping trips in 90+ degree temperatures, and it would never fail to whisk me away to the Andes.<br /><br />Here are my only two complaints.... First, I always skip the first chapter, which concerns the history of Uruguay, a description of rugby, and a history of Uruguayan rugby. These details are unnecessary and just delay the real story. Also, I could do with fewer accounts of the families' attempts to locate the survivors with the help of a psychic. These passages do at least let us spend some time with the families and let us know what they're going through, but of course the psychic accomplishes absolutely nothing and doesn't have a clue where the survivors are. Every time we visit the families and the psychic we are being taken away from the real story.<br /><br />But these flaws are minor and do nothing to lessen the amazingness of the story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5715188090075986064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3183474899" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3183474899" />
    <a title="Annie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38036772-annie"><img alt="Annie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586205974p2/38036772.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3183474899?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annie" href="/user/show/38036772-annie">Annie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3183474899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7576468593526334362">I know, I know. Am I on a cannibal kick or something? I just finished <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3114998575?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>this book on the Donner Party</a> a few weeks ago, now I’m reading <i>Alive</i> AND <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3183473296?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Miracle in the Andes</a>? (I’ll also throw in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1985147014?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cannibalism: A Perfectly Natural History</a>, though I read that a few years back). <br/><br/>...I’m sorry, what can I say? I’m <i>hungry</i>, okay? In the coronavirus chaos, it’s a dog eat dog world. <br/><br/>I’ll start off by saying that this is rather more satisfying a read than the Donner Party book, <i>the Indifferent Stars Above</i>—not because</span>
  <span id="freeText7576468593526334362" style="display:none">I know, I know. Am I on a cannibal kick or something? I just finished <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3114998575?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">this book on the Donner Party</a> a few weeks ago, now I’m reading <i>Alive</i> AND <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3183473296?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Miracle in the Andes</a>? (I’ll also throw in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1985147014?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Cannibalism: A Perfectly Natural History</a>, though I read that a few years back). <br /><br />...I’m sorry, what can I say? I’m <i>hungry</i>, okay? In the coronavirus chaos, it’s a dog eat dog world. <br /><br />I’ll start off by saying that this is rather more satisfying a read than the Donner Party book, <i>the Indifferent Stars Above</i>—not because the storytelling is any better (if anything, I think Daniel Brown was a better storyteller than Piers Paul Read), but simply because there’s so much more material to work from here. This book was published just two years after the disaster. He spoke to the survivors personally, and at great length. There are photographs of the survivors, both before the event and actually at the plane wreck (they had a camera). <br /><br />Whereas there are few photos of the members of the Donner Party (taken later in life), and none were actually at the camp. There aren’t many detailed, written accounts by the survivors. And, of course, the author could not ask them questions when he needed to flesh out the story a bit more (...pardon the grisly pun, it was absolutely intentional). <br /><br />Another note: after reading this, but before reading Nando Parrado’s memoir <i>Miracle in the Andes</i>, I watched the History Channel documentary <i>I Am Alive</i>, available for free on Youtube, narrated by Parrado and Canessa, among a few others. I found it very helpful for visualizing the area where the plane crashed, as well as just visualizing things in general. It also doesn’t get bogged down by talking about ALL of the passengers so it’s a little more narrative-friendly. <br /><br />Alive’s author tries to give a story to each of the passengers, including the ones who died, which I really appreciate from a human perspective--they were all important, they all deserve to be known. But from a narrative perspective it’s a little hard to keep track of everyone and not get bogged down in the details. <br /><br /><b>------------SUMMARY-----------</b><br /><br /><b>
  <u>Background:</u>
</b> A group of boys played rugby together at Stella Maris, a Catholic school in a wealthy suburb of Montevideo, Uruguay. As they grew up and graduated, they continued playing, and became a travelling rugby team (the players were all between the ages of 18 and 26). They booked a small charter flight for a game (and to party) in Santiago, Chile. The remaining tickets were sold to family and friends (either who wanted to watch the game, or who just wanted a cheap flight to Chile). The flight would cross through Uruguay, then Argentina and the Andes, then Chile. <br /><br />On Friday the 13, in October 1972, the flight crashed in the Andes. The pilots believed they had passed the Andes and were near Curico, Chile. Spoiler alert, they were not. As a result, the boys believed that they were in the foothills of the Andes and just over the next mountain were the green valleys of Chile. In fact, they were right in the cordillera, the heart of the Andes. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>Dramatis Personae:</u>
</b> There are a TON of people involved, but some stand out more than others and I’ll focus on those here. <br /><br />First and foremost, <b>Nando Parrado,</b> easily the most famous icon of the event. He was a rugby player, and his mother and sister Susana accompanied him. Parrado was the most beloved of the survivors, for his endless optimism, determination to escape, patience with his compatriots, and endless hard work—even when it wasn’t his job. In some ways, it seems like Parrado was born for this specific kind of disaster. <br /><br />Parrado was unconscious for days after the accident. When he woke up and learned his mother was dead, his only thought was to care for his severely injured and frostbitten younger sister, Susana--his fervor for her seemed to make him recover even more rapidly and he gained “a lunatic determination to escape.” He was the first to suggest eating the dead to sustain them--specifically to sustain them as they made their escape to find their own rescue. <br /><br />Then the two medical students, <b>Roberto Canessa</b> and <b>Gustavo Zerbino</b>. Canessa, along with Parrado, made the expedition west through the Andes, about 35 miles of scaling 10,000 foot mountains. Canessa is stubborn, with a temper, but with a kind heart and among the most inventive of the survivors. Zerbino is clever and funny, as well as coldly logical, and would have been a candidate for the expedition had he not suffered from snow blindness due to a previous, exploratory trip. <br /><br /><b>Marcelo Perez</b> was the selfless, generous-hearted team captain who immediately became the leader after the disaster. He was one of the 8 who died in an avalanche a few weeks after the accident.<br /><br /><b>Carlitos Paez</b> was a rather shrill hypochondriac, impatient but ultimately good-hearted. His father was a famous Uruguayan artist, Carlos Paez Vilaro. Vilaro was arguably one of the fiercest in his determination to find his son alive, of all the parents involved in the search when the Chilean government abandoned the search shortly after the accident. <br /><br /><b>The Methols— Javier and Liliana</b> were a couple, both about 10 years older than the rest of those who survived the initial crash. Javier (the cousin of one of the rugby players) was initially suffering from severe altitude sickness and had to be cared for by Liliana, though he ultimately survived the disaster. Liliana, the only woman who did not die in the accident or shortly thereafter, was an enormous source of comfort for the boys, elevated almost to a Virgin Mother status. Like the team captain Marcelo Perez, Liliana perished in the avalanche several weeks later. Javier and Liliana were the last to taste human meat, holding out for as long as they could. <br /><br /><b>Pancho Delgado</b> is an articulate law student, who initially calmed others with his serene optimism and his engaging stories; later, he was disliked by the rest of the group, for although he did not physically contribute much, he snuck extra food. <br /><br /><b>Fito Strauch, Eduardo Strauch, and Daniel Fernandez</b> were three cousins, so close they were more like brothers, who all survived together. After Marcelo Perez’s death, their unified front made them the leaders who organized the others into groups and assigned them tasks. <br /><br />Finally, my favourite is probably <b>Enrique Platero</b>. Platero was impaled by the plane crash on a steel tube, right through his stomach. Medical student Zerbino was appalled but didn't want to panic Platero. So he looked Platero straight in the eye and said, "Well, Enrique, you look all right." When Enrique points at his stomach like "What the fuck dude," Zerbino adds, "Don't worry about that. You're perfectly strong, so come and give me a hand." Platero just accepts this and goes to help someone--at which point Zerbino grabs the tube and pulls it out of Platero's stomach, along with about six inches of intestine. <br /><br />Then--and this is such a great description for so many reasons--Platero "contemplated his projecting innards with some dismay," but before he can react, Zerbino says, "Now look here, Enrique, you may think you're in a bad way, but there are plenty of others much worse off than you are, so don't be a coward, and come and help. Tie that up with a shirt and I'll see to it later." Enrique uncomplaining does what he's told and goes to help others. <br /><br />Later, Zerbino examines the hole and the bleeding intestine. Zerbino sews the hole in the intestine and disinfects it with COLOGNE, then tells Platero to push the intestines back in his own stomach-- which Platero does <i>without complaint.</i> <br /><br />Over the next week or so, the hole in his stomach starts to heal up and Platero informs Canessa that he was feeling "perfectly well" (that's some aggressive optimism wow) except that a piece of his insides was still sticking out of the wound. Some of the protruding guts were starting to die, so Canessa suggested that he could cut off that part (assuming it's part of the lining of the stomach) and try to push the rest back inside his body--but if it turned out to be intestine, Canessa told him that if he cut it, Enrique would die. As always, Enrique shrugs this off and without hesitation lets Canessa operate on him with a piece of glass, in the snow, without anesthesia. Canessa offers to stitch him up when he's done, but Enrique says "Don't worry, this is fine. Just tie it up again and I'll be on my way." <br /><br />Literally what the fuck. That is one resilient dude. <br /><br />The hole actually ended up healing just fine despite their circumstances, making it especially tragic when, like Marcelo Perez and Liliana Methol, he too perished in the avalanche. <br /><br /><b>---------THE ELEPHANT IN THE ROOM----------</b><br /><br />After a week or two of virtually no food, the 27 remaining survivors decided to eat the bodies of the death, after much debate. Honestly, I’m shocked by how long it took them to make this decision. Why is it even a decision? Humans are so weird! It’s really not that big of a deal. <br /><br />As Zerbino says, “I know that if my dead body could help you to stay alive, then I’d certainly want you to use it. In fact, if I do die, and you don’t eat me, then I’ll come back from wherever I am and give you a good kick in the ass.” SAME. <br /><br />One boy was near death buried beneath the avalanche (soon freed by his friends), “yet the knowledge that after his death his body would help the others to survive instilled in him a kind of ecstasy. It was as if he were already at the portals of heaven.”<br /><br />Some of the boys felt they would go to hell if they ate their dead friends though. Not Liliana; I can sympathize with her view much better. She did not believe it was morally wrong, she knew that social taboo was not a law of God, but her physical revulsion prevented her from doing it. <br /><br />Yeah, that’s fine. I get that, a lot more than I get the morality argument. But like, if you were starving… a choice between overcoming physical revulsion, and, you know DEATH… I think I’ll manage to get it down somehow. <br /><br />Anyway, initially they only ate the meaty parts—limbs, torsos—but eventually they would eat nearly all parts— the organs, the intestines, even eventually the brains, served in an uncooked stew inside bowls made from skulls.<br /><br /><b>--------THE EXPEDITION---------</b><br /><br />Nando Parrado, as soon as his sister died, wanted to set off for Chile to get back to his father and let him know he was still alive. The only thing they knew for sure— Chile was west of where they were. In fact, “to the west is Chile” because the group’s mantra for the duration of their time in the cordillera. <br /><br />The others, though, made Parrado wait until December, when the snow was less intense (remember, this is the Southern Hemisphere). After several failed expeditions, the successful one involved Parrado and Canessa (as well as initially a third member, Vizintin, though he returned after the first couple of days). <br /><br />In preparation for the expedition, the group sewed the travelers a communal sleeping bag from the plane’s insulation, packed meat in socks for their packs, and made them socks from the elbow and forearm skin of their dead friends. <br /><br />When Parrado, Canessa, and Vizintin make it to the top of the first and largest mountain (named Mount Seler by Parrado, for his father—the area is so remote that the 15,000 foot peak had never been climbed or named before), they were hoping/expecting to see the green valleys of Chile below. Instead, they saw more snow-covered mountains, save for two green mountains in the extreme distance (roughly 60 miles away). Accordingly, Parrado and Canessa send Vizintin back down the mountain to the plane fuselage, so that they can conserve the food they brought for the longer-than-expected journey.<br /><br />After 35 miles, Parrado and Canessa make it to a valley, where they spot a cow, and then a man on horseback. After throwing a rock with a note begging for his help, the man on horseback rides for 10 hours and returns with help. Parrado and Canessa are fed and put up for the night in a peasant’s home. The next day, helicopters come; Parrado rides with them to show them where the remaining 14 survivors are located. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7576468593526334362" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1946665472" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1946665472" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7524614-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404844794p2/7524614.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1946665472?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/7524614-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1946665472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17231457513125527243">Interesting, sad, and captivating. I had to read this for class in my senior year of high school. I have always been interested in books like these, of pure survival at all costs and the souls desperate scrabble to live. These men did what it took, and while it wrenched my stomach, as did many others, I understand why. </span>

          </span>
        </div>
